    Identification and Optimization of Pyrrolidine Derivatives as Highly Potent Ghrelin Receptor Full Agonists

    Abstract

    Muscle atrophy and cachexia are common comorbidities among patients suffering from cancer, chronic obstructive pulmonary disease, and several other chronic diseases. The peptide hormone ghrelin exerts pleiotropic effects including the stimulation of growth hormone secretion and subsequent increase of insulin-like growth factor-1 levels, an important mediator of muscle growth and repair. Ghrelin also acts on inflammation, appetite, and adipogenesis and therefore has been considered a promising therapeutic target for catabolic conditions. We previously reported on the synthesis and properties of an indane based series of ghrelin receptor full agonists which led to a sustained increase of insulin-like growth factor-1 in a dog pharmacodynamic study. Herein we report on the identification of a series of pyrrolidine or piperidine based full agonists and attempted optimization to give compounds with profiles suitable for progression as clinical candidates.

    Introduction

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    Ghrelin is a 28 amino acid hormone that is synthesized and secreted by X/A-like cells, often called ghrelin cells, localized within the oxyntic glands of the mucosa of the gastric fundus.(1) It is highly conserved across several species and broadly expressed indicating its physiological importance.(2,3) O-n-octanoylation on the serine 3 residue of ghrelin, mediated by the ghrelin O-acyl-transferase (GOAT), is required for binding to and activation of its cognate receptor, the type 1a growth hormone secretagogue receptor (GHS-R1a). GHS-R1a is a seven transmembrane G-protein-coupled receptor expressed predominantly in the hypothalamus and in the pituitary gland. Ghrelin mediates multiple biological activities, including stimulation of growth hormone (GH) secretion and effects on appetite and food intake, metabolism, inflammation, cardiac output, and gastric motility. Since its discovery, regulation of food intake has been the most studied function of this hormone. Many studies have been directed to find new ghrelin antagonists and inverse agonists to treat metabolic disorders related to obesity, such as type-2 diabetes mellitus.(4) This has proven to be a difficult task for the medicinal chemists since very small changes on the chemotype structure can have divergent pharmacological effects (partial-full agonist, antagonist, inverse agonist).(5,6) However, we were particularly interested in its ability to increase endogenous GH and elevate the plasma level of insulin-like growth factor-1 (IGF-1), both positive regulators of skeletal muscle hypertrophy, and consequently in its potential application to ameliorate muscle atrophy and cachexia. As common comorbidities in chronic diseases, such as cancer, chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD), chronic heart failure, and acquired immunodeficiency syndrome (AIDS), muscle atrophy and cachexia are associated with increased weakness and decreased functional capacity. The use of ghrelin to reduce cachexia and increase functional capacity in cachectic patients with COPD has clinical precedence.(7,8) In a previous paper,(9) we described the synthesis and characterization of a series of compounds, based on an indane scaffold, which were potent, full agonists at the ghrelin receptor. From this series, compounds that showed limited brain penetrance and long half-life were investigated for their ability to increase the IGF-1 secretion in dog, and in particular compound 1, with good in vivo efficacy and acceptable pharmacokinetics in the preclinical species, was selected for further evaluation. However, compound 1 suffered from activity on the μ1-opioid receptor, an off-target activity that made it unsuitable for further progression in the intended patient population. Thus, an alternative series was sought, which retained the key pharmacophore features of the indane series, but with improved selectivity and PK profiles that would make them suitable as potential clinical candidates. In this regard, we herein describe the design and synthesis of the potent full agonist compounds 2a and 3a with pyrrolidine and piperidine cores, respectively, Figure 1. Subsequently we describe the optimization of these new series, leading to the identification of the very selective compound 12j with excellent potency and a PK profile consistent with a low predicted dose to man.
    Unfortunately, due to a combination of a suboptimal permeability and P-glycoprotein (Pgp)-mediated efflux, oral absorption of compound 12j and related compounds was poor in rodent species which would have had an impact on attainable exposure during preclinical safety studies. Also, while bioavailability in the dog was much higher, considerable variability in exposure between animals was observed, and this, coupled with low permeability observed in human intestinal tissue, led to the decision not to progress 12j into preclinical development; however exploration of the pharmacology of this series of ghrelin receptor full agonists continued.

    Chemistry

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    Initial preparation of the pyrrolidine stereoisomers 2a and 4 was from the commercially available racemic trans 1-tert-butyl 3-ethyl 4-aminopyrrolidine-1,3-dicarboxylate 17, according to the procedure outlined in Scheme 1. Thus, coupling with 5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxylic acid using HATU and DIPEA in DMF gave amide 18 in excellent yield. Removal of the Boc group with TMSCl in EtOH followed by reaction with benzenesulfonyl chloride in the presence of pyridine gave sulfonamide 19. Subsequent hydrolysis of the ester with lithium hydroxide and coupling with N1,N1-dimethylethane-1,2-diamine, using HATU and DIPEA in DMF, gave predominately the racemic trans isomer 20 plus a minor isomeric product which was separated by RP preparative HPLC and identified as the cis-isomer 5. This was presumed to have arisen from epimerization α to the amide carbonyl during either the ester hydrolysis or the subsequent amide coupling step. The purified compound 20 was subjected to chiral HPLC to furnish the trans isomers 2a and 4. Stereochemical assignments were initially made based upon precedence for order of elution of related compounds and subsequently confirmed by comparison to 2a synthesized from the common intermediate 44.
    Initial synthesis of the piperidine compounds 3a, 6, and 7 was performed from the commercially available racemic (trans)-methyl 4-(tert-butoxycarbonylamino)piperidine-3-carboxylate 21 according to the procedure outlined in Scheme 2. Thus, reaction with benzenesulfonyl chloride in the presence of Et3N gave sulfonamide 22. Removal of the Boc group and coupling with 5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxylic acid using HATU and DIPEA in DMF proceeded smoothly to give the amide 23. Subsequent hydrolysis of the ester with lithium hydroxide and a second HATU coupling with N1,N1-dimethylethane-1,2-diamine gave predominately the racemic trans isomer 24 plus a minor isomeric product which, following separation by preparative HPLC and further purification by achiral SFC chromatography, was confirmed as the cis racemic isomer 7. The purified racemic mixture 24 was subjected to chiral preparative SFC chromatography to furnish the trans isomers 3a and 6. The relative stereochemistry for 24 and 7 was confirmed by NMR, and the absolute stereochemistry for 3a and 6 was confirmed by comparison to a second batch of 3a subsequently synthesized from the common chiral intermediate 25 prepared according to literature procedures.(10) Thus, coupling with 5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxylic acid using HBTU and Et3N in MeCN gave amide 26 in excellent yield. Removal of the Boc group with TFA provided the free piperidine 27 that by reaction with benzenesulfonyl chloride gave the sulfonamide ethyl ester 28. This was submitted to hydrolysis and subsequent coupling with N1,N1-dimethylethane-1,2-diamine in the presence of HBTU and Et3N in DMSO to give the enantiomerically pure compound 3a.
    The piperidine derivatives 3b–e and 3g were prepared according to a more convergent route as described in Scheme 3. Compound 32 was a common intermediate, some of the compounds were prepared and tested as racemates (3b, 3e, and 3g), while some racemic mixtures were submitted to chiral separation, and the pure enantiomers were tested (3c, 3d).
    From the chiral building block 27, the chiral compounds 3f, 11a, and 11b were made without use of chiral chromatography, following Scheme 4.
    Synthesis of the alternative piperidine regioisomers 8 and 9 was from the ethyl 3-oxopiperidine-4-carboxylate hydrochloride via the procedure outlined in Scheme 5. Thus, reaction of the oxopiperidine with benzenesulfonyl chloride in the presence of Et3N gave the sulfonamide 35. Condensation with NH4OAc gave the enamine 36 that was reduced with NaBH(OAc)3 in MeCN in the presence of acetic acid to give 37 as a mixture of diastereoisomers. Coupling with 5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxylic acid using HBTU and Et3N in DCM proceeded smoothly to give the amide 38 as a 4:1 mixture of cis:trans isomers. Hydrolysis of the ethyl ester with NaOH, followed by coupling with N1,N1-dimethylethane-1,2-diamine, HBTU, Et3N, in MeCN gave the diamide 39 that was submitted to preparative HPLC to afford the two racemic isomers, trans8 and cis9.
    For the synthesis of subsequent analogs of the pyrrolidine compound 2a, a common intermediate was sought that would enable library synthesis in which the two amide side-chains and the sulfonamide could be independently varied. Thus, compound 44 was designed, and the synthesis is outlined in Scheme 6. Formation of the enamine 41 from the readily available keto ester 40 and (S)-α-methylbenzylamine was accomplished in high yield following literature procedures.(11,12) Reduction of the enamine with sodium borohydride, which was known to occur with reasonable diastereoselectivity, gave 42 with the required 4R stereochemistry but predominately as the cis isomer. However, epimerization, using DBU in MeOH, converted this to a 3:1 mixture in favor of the required trans isomer. This ratio was further improved by conversion to the hydrochloride salt and crystallization to give 43 in >98% de. Removal of the α-methylbenzyl group, by catalytic hydrogenation over 10% Pd/C, furnished the key intermediate 44, isolated as the hydrochloride salt.(13,14)
    Synthesis of final compounds 2a–g and 10a–g could thus be efficiently achieved by varying the order of steps, according to methods A and B, as exemplified in Scheme 7. Additionally method C, which employs compound 43 as key intermediate as exemplified in Scheme 8, could be used to reduce the overall number of steps where it is desired to vary the aromatic amide substitution as in the case of the synthesis of compounds 12a–m.
    Method A: Amide coupling of compound 44 with aromatic acids gave compound 45, and subsequent ester hydrolysis followed by a second amide coupling gave intermediate 46. Removal of the Boc protective group and reaction with sulfonyl chlorides then furnished compounds 2a–g with varied sulfonamide substitutions.
    Method B: Removal of the Boc protection from 45 followed by reaction with benzenesulfonyl chloride gave intermediate 47 which could be subjected to ester hydrolysis and then to amide coupling to furnish compounds 10a–g.
    Method C: Removal of the Boc protection group from 43 and then reaction with benzenesulfonyl chloride could be accomplished with selective reaction on the pyrrolidine nitrogen to give intermediate 48. Standard ester hydrolysis followed by amide formation with mono Boc protected diamines yielded intermediate 49 with no acylation observed on the hindered secondary amine. Removal of the α-methylbenzyl protecting group proceeded slowly under catalytic hydrogenation and did not generally go to completion. However, the resulting mixture could be taken directly to the next stage as residual compound 49 did not react in the subsequent coupling reaction and could be removed by extraction into dilute hydrochloric acid. Finally, removal of the Boc protection proceeded smoothly to give final products 12a–m and 16b.
    In some cases, a degree of epimerization was observed during the hydrolysis step resulting in the formation of the cis isomers as byproducts which were often difficult to separate from the trans isomers by preparative HPLC. However, by performance of ester hydrolysis under acidic rather than basic conditions and replacing diisopropylamine with the less basic N-methylmorpholine in the amide coupling reactions, formation of cis isomers could be avoided.
    Methylation by formaldehyde of compound 12j led to compound 16d. Acylation with HCO2Et or Ac2O led to compounds 16e and 16f. In an analog manner to the synthesis of compounds 12a–m, also compounds 14, 15, and 16b were synthesized.
    The N-methylated compounds 16a and 16c were obtained by reductive methylation of compound 48 followed by hydrogenolysis of the α-methylbenzyl group and derivatization according to the procedure outlined in Scheme 9.
    For the synthesis of the α-methylated derivatives, such as 13b, it was necessary to build the pyrrolidine ring from scratch according to the method described in Scheme 10. Coupling of dimethyl 2-methylfumarate and N-benzyl-1-methoxy-N-((trimethylsilyl)methyl)methanamine, under acid catalysis via a [2 + 3] cycloaddition, gave the pyrrolidine diester 55 as a racemic mixture of trans isomers. Removal of the benzyl group by hydrogenolysis followed by reaction with benzenesulfonyl chloride gave compound 56. Selective monohydrolysis of the less-hindered ester of 56 to give 57b was attempted using 1 equiv of NaOH; however an inseparable mixture of 57a and 57b contaminated with diacid was obtained. This crude mixture was taken forward into the subsequent Curtius rearrangement step in which the initially formed isocyanates were reacted with benzyl alcohol to give a mixture of benzyl carbamates 58a and 58b. Hydrolysis of the remaining ester groups followed by the coupling of the resulting acids with tert-butyl (3S)-3-aminopiperidine-1-carboxylate and the hydrogenolysis of the benzylcarbamate gave a mixture of amides 59a and 59b. Amide coupling and removal of the Boc protective group furnished a mixture of 13a and 13b from which 13b (but not 13a) was isolated in pure form by preparative reverse-phase HPLC.

    Results and Discussion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    Much effort has been made to clarify the effects and mechanism of action of ghrelin in the CNS and peripheral tissues. The effect of ghrelin modulators as a function of their brain access is a topic still largely discussed in the literature for ghrelin receptors. It is still unclear if ghrelin needs to cross the blood–brain barrier (BBB) in order to have an effect. Since the GSH-R1a is also present on vagal afferents, some studies suggest that ghrelin stimulated food intake comes exclusively via the vagus nerve;(15) however other studies in rodents have shown that gut vagal afferents are not necessary for the orexigenic effect of peripherally injected ghrelin.(16) According to these studies, it is likely that both circulating ghrelin crossing the BBB and ghrelin coming directly from the stomach through the vagus nerve are responsible for the central actions.(17) On the basis of this information and due to the serious (CNS)-related side effects of a ghrelin agonist entering the brain, i.e., insomnia and addiction, we hypothesized that a full ghrelin agonist could be designed to show the desired effect on the GH/IGF-1 axis without penetrating the brain.
    We have previously reported the identification of the potent ghrelin full agonist compound 1 which was based on an indane scaffold.(9) This compound showed limited brain/plasma levels and high clearance in rat PK studies but an acceptable half-life in the dog. In a dog PD study, compound 1 produced a sustained increase of IGF-1, which supported our initial hypothesis that CNS penetration is not a requirement for affecting the GH/IGF-1 axis. Unfortunately, activity on the μ1-opioid receptor made the indane compound unsuitable as a potential drug candidate. Alternative structural classes that maintained the key pharmacophore features of the indane series were investigated.
    To explain structure–activity relationship of GHSR agonists, we used structure-based molecular modeling studies based on homology model of human GHSR built on the X-ray structure of the agonist-bound human adenosine A2A receptor (Brookhaven PDB code 3QAK) as described in our previous publication.(9) Ligand-binding pocket of adenosine receptors is known to be located in the middle of the 7TM bundle of helices along the long axis of the GPCR geometry.(18) This suits well to the long narrow shape of many small-molecule GHSR ligands.(5,19) However, the X-ray structure of the agonist-bound rat neurotensine receptor 1 was recently successfully used as the template for homology modeling of GHSR binding site for narrow long reverse agonists.(5) This challenged us to try and look at the structure–activity relationship of our small-molecule GHSR agonists retrospectively through the new homology model. Putative binding mode of GHSR agonists was obtained using a homology model of human GHSR based on the X-ray structure of agonist-bound rat neurotensine receptor 1 as a crystallographic template (PDB accession code 4BUO),(20) which shows 39.4% sequence identity with GHSR.(5) The choice of this template protein was made based on its highest sequence identity and sequence similarity with GHSR among GPCR proteins with resolved structures.(21) Since the purpose of our lead optimization efforts was to design GHSR agonists, it was crucial to create the homology model in active GPCR conformation.(22) The homology model was created by the program MOE (release 2019.01, Chemical Computing Group Inc.). Putative binding modes of the GHSR agonists under study in the GHSR homology model were defined by local energy minimization using high-quality all-atom OPLS3e force field(23) and GB/SA effective solvation model(24) as incorporated into MacroModel (Schrodinger LLC), which involved the bound agonist and nearby amino acid side chains of GHSR located closer than 7 Å from the ligand. Initial orientation of the GHSR agonists under study within the homology model prior to geometry optimization of the ligand-GHSR complex was obtained using residue E124, the recognized salt-bridge anchor for basic nitrogens of GHSR ligands,(25,5,19) while placing the conformationally rigid indole–carboxamide moiety of 1 and 2a along the long axis of the receptor toward the extracellular loops through residue R283.(25,26,5) The obtained binding modes of the GHSR agonists along with the long axis of the homology model and bound ligands are shown in Figure 2. Despite differences in structure and pharmacology, the binding mode of our agonists within the homology model of GHSR shares eight neighboring residues with that of the inverse agonist compound 47 described by Daina et al., namely, E124, R199, Q120, R283, F279, F286, F309, and F312.(5) Residues S123, E124, and F279 have been defined as the hottest binding hits in a recent multidisciplinary study of binding of a peptidomimetic agonist G-7039 to a homology model of GHSR.(21) In addition, important residues of the identified binding site F119, Q120, S123, E124, R199, F279, R283, F286, and F309 have been demonstrated to be the strongest mutational hits for binding of a number of agonists.(27−29)
    Our lead optimization strategy was to investigate other cores that would retain the binding mode and conformation of the two amide side-chains of indanes. The aim was to enhance the attractive nondesolvation-related interactions of the ligand with nearby residues of the ghrelin receptor. Contribution of these interactions to the binding energy of buried ligand can be assessed by the value of ligand-lipophilicity efficiency, LLE = pIC50 – log D.(30) The increase of LLE during lead optimization stage is essential as this would prevent more potent compounds from becoming too hydrophobic, which would lead to multiple unwanted side effects.(31) Correspondingly, potency of a better ghrelin receptor agonist was expected to be based on a balance of hydrophobic and polar interactions of the new core with the binding site. Molecular modeling of putative ligand–receptor complex suggested that neighboring polar residue S123 could likely be reached by the new cores. Of particular interest were the pyrrolidine and piperidine cores with phenyl sulfonamide substitution, as in 2a and 3a, since overlay of 2a with compound 1 (Figure 2) showed a particularly good overlap of the key pharmacophore features for both compounds, while the phenyl ring was expected to make new hydrophobic interactions with residues M96, L100, F309, F312, and Y313, and the sulfonyl group a new H-bond with S123; hence these compounds were selected for synthesis. For comparison, pyrrolidine compounds 4 (enantiomer of 2a) and 5 (racemic cis isomer), piperidine compounds 6 (enantiomer of 3a) and 7 (racemic cis isomer), and the alternative piperidine regioisomers 8 (racemic trans isomer) and 9 (racemic cis isomer) were also synthesized and tested, Table 1. As the optimized indane compound 1 had the LLE value of 6, a significant increase of this value for compounds with the new cores was expected as a sign of new attractive polar interactions with the ghrelin receptor.
    Table 1. Binding, Functional Assay Potency, Agonism (%), log D, and LLE of Compounds 1, 2a–3a, 4–9

    a

    In vitro assay. Binding assay to detect compounds binding to the GHS-R1a expressed in HEK293 cells, by competing with [125I]-ghrelin. Values are the mean of at least 2 independent determinations.

    b

    In vitro assay. Agonist activation of the ghrelin receptor results in a production of IP1. IP1 is accumulated in the cell in the presence of LiCl and measured by time-resolved fluorescence. Values are the mean of at least 2 independent determinations. The assay validation resulted in a CIR value of ±0.07 and an MDR value of 0.1 (on a log scale) when tested at 2 separate occasions.

    c

    Distribution coefficient expressing partitioning of compounds between 1-octanol and aqueous buffer at pH 7.4.

    d

    Ligand-lipophilicity efficiency (pIC50 – log D).

    Gratifyingly, both the pyrrolidine compound 2a and the piperidine compound 3a, with lower log D, were found to be full agonists, equipotent in the binding assay and more potent in the functional assay than the indane compound 1. The trans and cis isomers of the alternative piperidine regioisomers, compounds 8 and 9, were less potent with reduced agonism. As expected, the enantiomeric compounds 4 and 6 as well as the cis racemates 5 and 7 were all less potent, and all, except 7, had reduced agonism.
    Initial attempts to optimize the lead compounds 2a and 3a focused upon replacements within the phenyl sulfonamide core, Table 2. As seen in Figure 2, the subpocket that encloses this core is of a hydrophobic nature with only one polar residue, S123, that likely H-bonds sulfonamide oxygen. This means changes within the aromatic moiety of the core are not expected to affect the values of LLE significantly, whereas the structure of the sulfonamide linker is likely essential. If the homology model and the hypothetical binding mode describe important features of the ligand–receptor interactions, this trend has to be retained. Results of Table 2 are indeed consistent with the structure of hypothesized subpocket as the compound potency is driven by lipophilicity with the exception of compound 3g with the amide linker, which does not fit. Residue S123 has previously been defined as the most important for free energy of binding of peptidomimetic agonist G-7039 with GHSR(21) and furthermore together with F119 constitutes an efficacy switch region.(21,28)
    Table 2. Binding, Functional Assay Potency, Agonism (%), log D, LLE, Rat Hepatocytes Clearance, and Rat PK Data of Compounds 2a–g and 3a–g

    Among the compounds tested in rat pharmacokinetic studies, a significant lack of correlation between in vitro and in vivo clearance was observed. Consistently, the effective in vivo half-lives of this series were shorter than predicted with a lower exposure (as measured by area under the curve) consistent with a higher in vivo clearance. This was a general trend for all compounds in the series. The reason behind this in vivo clearance underprediction was not due to an extrahepatic clearance mechanism but rather to a misleading experimental underestimation of in vitro Clint values. The poor intrinsic permeability of these compounds coupled with large efflux values (Pgp transporters) caused the compound uptake into the hepatocyte cells to become the rate limiting step (as opposite to the metabolism rate itself) which caused the false impression of compounds being stable and the consequent bad IVIV correlation.
    Consequently, in an effort to improve metabolic stability, initial focus was placed on replacements for the dimethylaminoethyl side-chain, whereby the basic nitrogen is incorporated into a ring, and maintaining the two-carbon link between the basic nitrogen and the amide nitrogen. This strategy led to the identification of (pyrrolidin-2-yl)methyl and 3-piperidyl as potent replacements of the acyclic amino side-chain, and a series of related pyrrolidine (10a–g) and piperidine (11a,b) compounds were prepared and tested, Table 3. These compounds are generally very potent, have higher LLE values than indanes, and are full agonists when the basic nitrogen is methylated or is the secondary amine. There is more than 10-fold difference in potency observed between the unmethylated 3-piperidyl epimers (10f and 10g) but less difference between the methylated isomers (10d and 10e) as also is observed between the methylated (pyrrolidin-2-yl)methyl isomers (10b and 10c). The N-alkylation on the piperidine nitrogen atom does not change the functional activity of this series of compounds. This is in contrast to what was observed on a piperidine substituted quinazoline series deeply investigated for the treatment of diabetes and obesity with ghrelin antagonists.(33) In general, intrinsic metabolic clearance, measured in rat hepatocytes and human microsomes, is significantly lower for the unmethylated secondary amines relative to the analogous methylated tertiary amines. In particular the (S)-3-piperidyl compound 10g in the pyrrolidine series has an attractive profile with excellent potency and good in vitro metabolic stability. However, in view of poor in vivo PK observed for earlier compounds, permeability in Caco-2 cells was determined for several compounds, and these data consistently showed low permeability (PappAtB) and high efflux ratios, with piperidines not showing any permeability improvement over the pyrrolidines.
    Table 3. Functional Assay Potency, Agonism (%), log D, LLE, Rat Hepatocytes, and Human Liver Microsomes Clearance and Permeability Data of Compounds 10a–g and 11a,b
    a–d

    See footnotes b–e in Table 1.

    See footnote f in Table 2.

    In vitro clearance in human liver microsomes ((μL/min)/mg).

    g

    Apparent permeability across human Caco-2 cell monolayers at pH 7.4: PappAtB is apparent permeability coefficient in apical to basolateral direction; PappBtA is apparent permeability coefficient in basolateral to apical direction (1 × 10–6 cm/s).

    In view of the excellent potency and good metabolic stability of compound 10g, the main focus was placed on the pyrrolidine series with (S)-3-piperidylamides. While compound 10g did not show significant inhibition against a panel of cytochrome P450 enzymes (IC50 > 30 μM for isoforms 3A4, 2D6, 2C9, 2C19 and equal to 9 μM for 1A2), there was nevertheless concern that metabolism of the indole group might be associated with reactive metabolite formation which could give rise to idiosyncratic liver toxicity.(34) Indeed, covalent adducts were detected when compound 10g was incubated with human microsomes in the presence of glutathione, which indicated the likely formation of reactive peroxide and/or benzoquinone imine metabolites.(35) Therefore, an alternative to the indole group of 10g was sought and a series of monocyclic and biaryl replacements were prepared, Table 4.
    Table 4. Functional Assay Potency, Agonism (%), log D, LLE, Rat Hepatocytes and Human Liver Microsomes Clearance, Permeability, Efflux Ratio, and Solubility Data of Compounds 12a–m
    a–g

    See footnotes in Table 3.

    h

    Efflux ratio is PappBtA/PappAtB.

    i

    Solubility determined from dried-DMSO stock solutions in aqueous buffer at pH = 7.4.(36)

    Initially five-membered monocyclic replacements for indole were investigated, and in general these were polar molecules with weak potency. Methylthiazole 12a and 3-cyclopropylpyrazole 12b were tolerated but quite weak, and the N-alkylated imidazole 12c was inactive. The loss of activity of 12c is explained by the significant drop in log D caused by basicity of imidazole. More significant increases in potency were observed upon addition of a phenyl ring as in 12d. Thus, a series of biaryl compounds with varied five-membered rings and substituted phenyl groups was investigated. These compounds were generally potent, and it was observed that addition of a 2-chloro substituent on the phenyl ring led to a significant increase in potency as exemplified by matched pairs 12d/12j and 12i/12k. Compound 12i, the 4-chloro analogue of 12j, was less potent. Molecular modeling studies suggest that the potent-boosting effect of the 2-chloro substituent in 12j, 12k, and 12m is due to the out-of-plane effect of this group on the conformation of the conjugated biaryl moiety, which results in attractive cation−π interaction of the 2-chlorophenyl with R199 of GHSR (Figure 3). The Caco-2 permeability values were low for all compounds in this series. For the biaryl compounds, very high efflux ratios were observed, whereas for the less lipophilic monocyclic compounds, efflux ratios were lower, but this did not reflect in an increased apparent permeability. Additionally, for many of the biaryl compounds, aqueous solubility was lower to the extent to reduce bioavailability, and unsurprisingly all compounds tested in rat PK had no or negligible bioavailability, Table 5. However, acceptable bioavailability was in general consistently observed for compounds tested in dog with the difference between the species being attributed to a decreased expression of Pgp in the gastrointestinal tract of our dogs.(37) Long half-lives were associated with low clearances and high volumes of distribution in both species.

    Compounds 12d and 12j were selected for further evaluation to assess improvements in the safety profile. Both compounds were found to have a clean secondary pharmacology profile against a broad counterscreening panel (CEREP), while, unlike for the indole compounds, covalent adducts were not detected following incubations with human microsomes in the presence of glutathione, indicating a reduced risk of hepatotoxicity. Compounds 12d and 12j also showed reduced interactions with μ- and κ-opiate receptors compared to the initial lead compound 1, Table 6.

    On the basis of the overall profile, compounds 12d and 12j were profiled further, Table 7. Compound 12j showed high potency and full agonism also in HEK293 cells transfected with dog ghrelin receptor and was therefore selected to be studied in the dog PK/PD model.

    This model was designed to determine the increase of IGF-1 and duration of effect while simultaneously providing pharmacokinetic data. This study compared the effect on IGF-1 plasma levels over 48 h following single doses of 0.8 mg/kg and 3 mg/kg by oral gavage to five dogs, and results are outlined, Figure 4. For the higher dose, >45% increase in IGF-1 compared to the t = 0 value was observed for the full duration of the experiment which correlated with sustained free plasma levels above EC50 (dotted line), based upon EC50 = 0.9 nM in the dog IP-1 assay and after adjusting for 3.5% free fraction in dog plasma. For the lower dose, a sustained but lower magnitude increase in IGF-1 was observed which returned to close to normal levels after a further 48 h (not shown). These data support the hypothesis that a ghrelin agonist with a long half-life will produce a dose-related sustained increase in IGF-1 and identifies 12j as a potential tool compound to investigate the effects of sustained rather than pulsed increases in IGF-1 on muscle wasting.
    On the basis of the favorable in vitro and in vivo profile already discussed, compound 12j became the front-runner in the selection of potential preclinical development candidates. However, there were inevitable concerns over the poor oral absorption in rodent (hamster, mouse, and rat) species and the implications for achieving adequate exposure in toxicology studies. Moreover, while bioavailability in dogs was much higher, greater than 2-fold dose normalized AUC, variability between animals was subsequently observed with a trend for lower exposure in females compared to males, Figure 5.
    Thus, in view of a perceived risk of poor and variable absorption in humans, an Ussing chamber experiment was conducted to measure permeability of compounds 12d and 12j across human and rat jejunum tissue. The results of these experiments are outlined in Figure 6 and show both compounds to be strongly effluxed with low apparent permeabilities (Papp) < 1 × 10–6 cm/s. Upon the basis of a comparison with human jejunal Papp data for known clinical compounds,(38) for which the fraction absorbed is known from human PK experiments, such low Papp values would predict for low (<10%) fraction absorbed in humans.
    Thus, on the basis of predicted poor absorption in humans, compound 12j was not progressed to preclinical development. However, attempts were made to identify either possible prodrugs or derivatives of 12j with improved passive permeability but still avoiding brain exposure at the same time. The main approaches were based on (i) reduction of the number of H-bond acceptors through replacement of the sulfonamide with an amide link or directly linked aromatics, but no increase of permeability was observed (unpublished data), (ii) modulation of H-bond strength through increased steric hindrance (13b), or (iii) reduction of the number of H-bond donors through incorporation into a ring (14), methylation (16a–d), or acylation (16e,f), Tables 8 and 9.

    Compound 13b, with the methyl substituent α to the amide carbonyl, was synthesized with the indole rather than the biaryl group of 12j to avoid increase of lipophilicity. Comparison with the corresponding analog 10g indicates nearly a 100-fold reduction in potency as a result of the methylation, which is probably due to altered relative conformation of the two amide side-chains. The compounds 14 and 15 retained good potency and both had improved, although Caco-2 AtB permeability was still low and the increased clearance in human microsomes was a concern for these compounds.
    Regrettably the efflux properties were not altered by the methylation of the amide nitrogens, as in 16a–c, and permeability was not significantly improved. Methylation of the amide linking of the piperidine ring also had a significant impact on potency as can be seen with 16b and 16c. Consistent with previous observations for compounds 10b,c,d,e and 11a, methylation of the basic secondary amine as in 16d was associated with increased potency but also decreased metabolic stability as was evident by higher intrinsic clearance in rat hepatocytes and in human microsomes and hepatocytes. The acylated compounds 16e and 16f also had higher intrinsic clearances as well as significantly reduced potency and, as expected, had a much better apparent (Caco PappAtB ∼ 1 1 × 10–6 cm/s) and intrinsic permeability (Caco PappAtB with efflux inhibited ∼70 1 × 10–6 cm/s). These compounds 16d–f were also considered as potential prodrugs if resulting in formation of 12j as the main metabolite (compounds 16a–c were too stable to be considered as valid prodrugs alternatives).
    Thus, a metabolite identification study was performed to compare metabolic pathways of 16d–f with 12j in rat, dog, and human hepatocytes (Table 10). The compounds were incubated in the presence of either fresh rat or dog hepatocytes or cryopreserved human hepatocytes for 120 min (16e and 16f were incubated in rat hepatocytes for 40 min), and main metabolites were identified by LCMSMS. Compounds observed were 12j, metabolites resulting from oxidation of the piperidine ring (identified as M – 2 or M + 16), and unidentified metabolites resulting from reactions of the remainder of the molecule (observed M + 323 or M – 124). The results show varying degrees of conversion of parent compound with formation of the desired 12j as a major metabolite in all cases except in the case of the acetyl derivative 16f, where 12j was not detected after the incubations in hepatocytes in any of the species.

    Thus, in view of in vitro evidence supporting potential formation of 12j as a major metabolite, compounds 16d–f were tested in in vivo rat PK experiments, with simultaneous analysis for the parent and the active compound 12j, Figure 7. Results for 16d and 16e show a significant increase of absorption of parent compound corresponding to a bioavailability of 9% and 4% and a Fabs of 13% and 10%, respectively, which is in agreement with the observed increased in permeability, and despite being still low, it is a major improvement compared to the undetectable bioavailability (<1%) of compound 12j. However, levels of the desired metabolite 12j were well below those of the parent with AUC0–t for the metabolite corresponding to 6% and 19% of the parent AUC0-t for the iv and oral doses, respectively.
    In similar experiments, 16e had 4% bioavailability with metabolite AUC0–t corresponding to 1% and 8% of parent AUC0–t for iv and oral doses, respectively, while 16f had <1% bioavailability, and in agreement with the results from the metabolic study performed previously in vitro rat hepatocytes, no levels of the active metabolite 12j were detected. Thus, on the basis of these results, although an improvement in bioavailability was observed for 16d and 16e, they were considered too metabolically stable to be developed as potential prodrugs for 12j. Research is ongoing to identify a more permeable compound that could allow us to make a more robust dose to human predictions.

    Conclusion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    We have reported the discovery of a new series of pyrrolidine and piperidine bis-amides as ghrelin full agonists that were derived from a previously reported indane series. Optimization of compounds in this series led to the identification of the pyrrolidine compound 12j with sub-nM in vitro potency, long half-life in rat and dog PK, sustained dose-dependent activity in a dog IGF-1 model, and a safety profile suitable for progression to preclinical development. However, compounds in this series are generally good substrates for efflux transporters as was evident from the low AtB permeability in Caco-2 cells and the negligible oral bioavailability in rat PK. Some compounds did nevertheless have much higher oral bioavailability in dogs, although this was variable and gender dependent. The potential impact of efflux upon intestinal absorption in humans is of key importance, and based upon experiments using human jejunum tissue, predicted human bioavailability for 12j was too low for it to be considered as a suitable clinical candidate.
    Attempts to improve bioavailability, primarily by reducing H-bonding potential and hence increasing the intrinsic permeability and diminishing the substrate potential of the compounds against Pgp transporters, led to identification of derivatives 16d–f with higher in vitro permeability. These compounds had high in vitro clearance, with 12j identified as a major metabolite in rat, dog, and human hepatocytes; hence they were considered as potential prodrugs for 12j rather than drug candidates in their own right. However, while low but significant bioavailability was observed in rat PK studies, detected levels of 12j were too low for these compounds to be progressed as prodrugs. Nevertheless, these studies highlighted the effectiveness of reducing H-bonding potential, in terms of improving bioavailability. Hence, the focus was put on modifications to the core structure that will facilitate bigger changes in this regard, and these results will be reported in due course.

    Experimental Section

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    General Chemistry

    All solvents and chemicals purchased from commercial suppliers where used as received. All reactions involving air- or moisture-sensitive reagents were performed under a nitrogen atmosphere using dry solvents. Proton (1H) and carbon (13C) NMR spectra were recorded at room temperature, unless otherwise specified. Solutions were typically prepared in deuterated dimethyl sulfoxide (DMSO-d6), deuterated chloroform (CDCl3), or deuterated methanol (CD3OD) with chemical shifts referenced to solvent as an internal standard. 1H NMR data are reported indicating the chemical shift (δ), the multiplicity (s, singlet; d, doublet; t, triplet; q, quartet; p, quintet; m, multiplet; br, broad; dd, doublet of doublets; etc.), the coupling constant (J) in Hz, and the integration (e.g., 1H). Silica gel chromatography was carried out using silica gel 60 (0.040–0.063 mm, Merck) or prepacked Biotage KP-SIL. Cation/anion exchange columns were Isolute SCX-2, Isolute CBA, and Isolute NH2. Preparative HPLC was performed with a Waters Fraction Lynx system with integrated MS detection or Gilson GX-281 with integrated UV detection using Waters Sunfire C18 OBD 5 μm, XBridge BEH C18 OBD 5 μm, Xselect CSH C18 OBD 5 μm or Kromasil C8 10 μm columns. Preparative chiral HPLC was performed with a Waters FractionLynx system with integrated MS detection or Gilson GX-281 with integrated UV detection using Chiralpak IB, Chiralpak IA, or Chiralpak IB IC 5 μm and Lux cellulose-4 5 μm columns. Preparative SFC was performed with a Waters Prep100 SFC system with integrated MS detection using CelluCoat column, particle size 5 μm, mobile phase 30% EtOH/DEA 100/0.5 in CO2, 150 bar or 25% or 30% MeOH/DEA 100/0.5 in CO2, 120 bar, flow 80 or 100 mL/min, detection 290–310 nm, temperature 40 °C. All compounds were purified to ≥95% purity as judged by HPLC with UV and MS analysis. LCMS was run on either a Waters Acquity TQD with a Waters Acquity UPLC LC/MS/MS system or Agilent HP 1100 LC/MS system, both equipped with a diode array UV detector from 220 to 350 nm and a mass spectrometer (ESI or APCI) with the following conditions: Waters Acquity UPLC BEH C18 column, elution gradient with a flow rate of 1 mL/min, and 2–95% acetonitrile in an aqueous 46 mM ammonium carbonate/ammonia buffer at pH 10, Waters Acquity UPLC HSS C18 column 1.8 μm, elution gradient with a flow rate of 1 mL/min, and 2–95% acetonitrile in an aqueous 10 mM formic acid/1 mM ammonium formate buffer at pH 3, or Waters Symmetry C18 column 3.5 μm, 2.1 mm × 30 mm, elution gradient with a flow rate of 0.7 mL/min, and 15–95% acetonitrile in water with 0.1% trifluoroacetic acid over 2.7 min and then 95% for 0.3 min. Chiral analyses were performed by following these SFC conditions: CelluCoat column, 250 mm × 4.6 mm, particle size 5 μm, mobile phase 30% EtOH/DEA 100/0.5 in CO2, 150 bar, flow 4 mL/min, detection 290 nm, temperature 40 °C. Accurate mass was determined using HRMS experiments performed on a Waters Acquity, Waters 2777, or Waters 2700 UV–HPLC system and a Waters Xevo G2 TOF, Waters LCT Premiere, Waters LCT, or Waters QTOF micro mass spectrometer. Optical rotation was measured with an ADP600 polarimeter system at the sodium D-line of 589 nm at specified conditions.

    N-((3R,4S)-4-((2-(Dimethylamino)ethyl)carbamoyl)-1-(phenylsulfonyl)pyrrolidin-3-yl)-5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxamide (2a), Second Procedure

    (3S,4R)-tert-Butyl 3-((2-(dimethylamino)ethyl)carbamoyl)-4-(5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxamido)pyrrolidine-1-carboxylate 46 (36 mg, 0.08 mmol) was dissolved in MeOH (2 mL), and TMSCl (0.01 mL, 0.08 mmol) was added to this at 0 °C. The mixture was allowed to warm to rt and stirred overnight. The solution was concentrated in vacuo and then dissolved in DCM (2 mL). Pyridine (0.041 mL, 0.50 mmol) was added and the mixture cooled to 0 °C. Benzenesulfonyl chloride (44.5 mg, 0.25 mmol) was then added, and the mixture was stirred at rt for 16 h. EtOAc was added and the solution washed with sat. NaHCO3 and brine. After concentration of the solution, the crude residue was purified by column chromatography (EtOAc/heptane 30%), which afforded compound 2a (7.2 mg, 18%) whose NMR spectrum was overlapping with the NMR obtained from the corresponding product synthesized from compound 17 in Scheme 1. Representative chemistry is reported for the synthesis of compound 2a. Similar procedure was applied to the synthesis of compounds 2b–g.

    N-((3S,4S)-3-((2-(Dimethylamino)ethyl)carbamoyl)-1-(phenylsulfonyl)piperidin-4-yl)-5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxamide (3a, Second Procedure)

    (3S,4S)-Ethyl-4-(5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxamido)-1-(phenylsulfonyl)piperidine-3-carboxylate 28 (0.75 g, 1.60 mmol) was dissolved in THF (20 mL) to give a colorless solution. To the stirred solution 1 M NaOH (8 mL, 8.00 mmol) was added, and the reaction mixture was heated with stirring at 40 °C for 4 h. The reaction mixture was then acidified by the addition of 1 M HCl (9 mL). THF was removed in vacuo and the product precipitated from the residual aqueous solution. The product was isolated by filtration, washed with water on the filter, to afford the acid intermediate (0.65 g) as a white solid. Mass spectrum (ESI) m/e = 442 (M + H)+. This acid (200 mg, 0.45 mmol) was then dissolved in DMSO (3 mL), and Et3N (95 μL, 0.68 mmol) was added to give a colorless solution. To the stirred solution, HBTU (189 mg, 0.50 mmol) and N1,N1-dimethylethane-1,2-diamine (43.9 mg, 0.50 mmol) were added, and the mixture was stirred for 30 min. The crude mixture was purified on preparative HPLC to give a compound (0.09 g) as a white solid. The material had 95% ee and identical optical rotation to the [α]589 = +119° obtained above (3a). The compounds were also proven identical by co-injection on the same analytical chiral SFC system used previously for separating the enantiomers and by 1H NMR.

    Ethyl 3-(5-Methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxamido)-1-(phenylsulfonyl)piperidine-4-carboxylate (38, Mixtures of Cis/Trans Isomers)

    Ethyl 3-amino-1-(phenylsulfonyl)piperidine-4-carboxylate 37 (0.32 g, 1.02 mmol) was dissolved in DCM (15 mL) to give a colorless solution. 5-Methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxylic acid (0.179 g, 1.02 mmol), HBTU (0.427 g, 1.13 mmol), and finally Et3N (0.357 mL, 2.56 mmol) were added to the stirred mixture. The reaction was stirred for 60 min, and the crude reaction mixture was purified on silica, giving 420 mg of an off white solid. The solid was slurried in heptane:EtOAc 2:1, and the suspension was stirred for 20 min before the solid was isolated by filtration and washed on the filter with Et2O, giving compound 38 (0.36 g, 74%) as a fine, white solid. The white solid contained a 4:1 mixture of the cis:trans according to LC–MS and 1H NMR. Mass spectrum (ESI) m/e = 470 (M + H)+.

    N-(4-((2-(Dimethylamino)ethyl)carbamoyl)-1-(phenylsulfonyl)piperidin-3-yl)-5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxamide (39, Mixtures of Isomers)

    Ethyl 3-(5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxamido)-1-(phenylsulfonyl)piperidine-4-carboxylate 38 (0.33 g, 0.70 mmol) was dissolved in THF (15 mL) to give a colorless solution. To the stirred solution, 1 M NaOH (3.51 mL, 3.51 mmol) and water (5 mL) were added. The mixture was stirred for 2 h at rt and the reaction was then acidified with 1 M HCl (4 mL), giving precipitation of product, which was extracted into EtOAc (2 × 30 mL). The organic phases were then washed with brine and dried over Na2SO4. Filtration and evaporation gave 0.31 g of the corresponding acid, as a mixture of cis:trans isomers. The acid (0.32 g, 0.72 mmol) and HBTU (0.330 g, 0.87 mmol) were added in MeCN (10 mL), and to this stirred solution N1,N1-dimethylethane-1,2-diamine (0.077 g, 0.87 mmol) and Et3N (0.303 mL, 2.17 mmol) were added. The reaction mixture was stirred for 1 h and then concentrated in vacuo to give the crude product 39.

    (4R)-1-tert-Butyl 3-Methyl 4-(((S)-1-phenylethyl)amino)pyrrolidine-1,3-dicarboxylate (42)

    (S)-1-tert-Butyl 3-methyl 4-((1-phenylethyl)amino)-1H-pyrrole-1,3(2H,5H)-dicarboxylate 41 (12.99 g, 37.50 mmol) in MeCN (90 mL) was treated with acetic acid (19.32 mL, 337.48 mmol). The reaction mixture was cooled to −9 °C, and NaBH4 (4.26 g, 112.49 mmol) was added in small portions over 40 min. The reaction mixture was allowed to warm to 10 °C. Further NaBH4 (2.128 g, 56.25 mmol) was added in two portions and the reaction was stirred between 0 and 10 °C for 20 h. To this, EtOAc (150 mL), water (100 mL), and 2 M NaOH (100 mL) were then added at 0 °C. The layers were separated, and the aqueous phase was extracted with EtOAc. The combined organic layers were washed with brine and dried over Mg2SO4. The volatiles were evaporated off and coevaporated several times with toluene to give compound 42 (12.99 g, 99%). Mass spectrum (ESI) m/e = 349 (M + H)+.

    (3S,4R)-1-tert-Butyl 3-Methyl-4-(((S)-1-phenylethyl)amino)pyrrolidine-1,3-dicarboxylate, Hydrochloride Salt (43)

    (4R)-1-tert-Butyl 3-methyl 4-(((S)-1-phenylethyl)amino)pyrrolidine-1,3-dicarboxylate 42 (12.3 g, 35.30 mmol) in MeOH (100 mL) was treated with DBU (2.64 mL, 17.65 mmol) and heated at 50 °C for 4 h. The mixture was allowed to cool to rt, and MeOH was removed under reduced pressure. EtOAc and 4% NaHCO3 were added. The layers were separated, and the aqueous phase was extracted with EtOAc. The combined organic layers were washed with brine, dried over Mg2SO4, and evaporated in vacuo. The residue was dissolved in EtOAc (140 mL) and cooled in an ice/water bath. 4 M HCl in dioxane (8.21 mL, 32.83 mmol) was then added dropwise over 5 min. The reaction mixture was allowed to stir at 0–3 °C for 1 h. The resulting solid was filtered off and recrystallized from MeCN to afford compound 43 as hydrochloride salt (4.60 g). 1H NMR (500 MHz, DMSO-d6) δ 1.37 (s, 9H), 1.64 (d, J = 6.6 Hz, 3H), 3.36–3.52 (m, 3H), 3.62 (s, 3H), 3.67–3.81 (m, 3H), 4.46–4.58 (m, 1H), 7.37–7.50 (m, 3H), 7.63 (d, J = 7.2 Hz, 2H), 9.96–10.28 (m, 2H).

    (3S,4R)-1-tert-Butyl 3-Methyl-4-aminopyrrolidine-1,3-dicarboxylate (44)

    (3S,4R)-1-tert-Butyl 3-methyl 4-(((S)-1-phenylethyl)amino)pyrrolidine-1,3-dicarboxylate, hydrochloride salt 43 (2.1 g, 5.45 mmol) was dissolved in DCM, and 1 M NaOH solution was added until the solution reached pH 10. The aqueous phase was back-extracted with DCM, and the combined organic phases were washed with brine and dried through a phase separator affording the free amine. This amine was dissolved in EtOH (20 mL), and 10% Pd/C (0.3 g, 2.82 mmol) was added. The compound was hydrogenated at 4 bar and rt for 20 h. The Pd catalyst was removed by filtration through Celite, the volatiles were removed in vacuo, and the residue was coevaporated 3 times with toluene to afford compound 44 (1.29 g, 84%) as a light brownish oil. 1H NMR (500 MHz, DMSO-d6) δ 1.39 (s, 9H), 2.11 (s, 2H), 2.73–2.84 (m, 1H), 2.91 (q, J = 9.1 Hz, 1H), 3.29–3.38 (m, 1H), 3.41–3.52 (m, 2H), 3.52–3.59 (m, 1H), 3.63 (s, 3H). Mass spectrum (ESI) m/e = 245 (M + H)+.

    (3S,4R)-1-tert-Butyl 3-Methyl-4-(5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxamido)pyrrolidine-1,3-dicarboxylate (45)

    A mixture of 5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxylic acid (2.04 g, 11.64 mmol), (3S,4R)-1-tert-butyl 3-methyl-4-aminopyrrolidine-1,3-dicarboxylate 44 (2.8 g, 11.46 mmol) and N-methylmorpholine (3.78 mL, 34.39 mmol) in DMF (25 mL) was treated with HATU (4.79 g, 12.61 mmol). The reaction mixture was stirred at rt overnight under nitrogen. The reaction mixture was then partitioned between EtOAc and ∼4% aqueous NaHCO3. The layers were separated, and the aqueous phase was back-extracted with EtOAc. The combined organic layers were washed with aqueous NaHCO3 and brine. The organic layer was dried over Na2SO4 and evaporated to dryness. The residue was purified by column chromatography (30–40% EtOAc/heptane) to afford compound 45 (4.07 g, 88%). 1H NMR (500 MHz, DMSO-d6) δ 1.55 (s, 9H), 2.50 (s, 3H), 3.28–3.43 (m, 2H), 3.57–3.67 (m, 1H), 3.76 (s, 3H), 3.78–3.87 (m, 2H), 4.75–4.87 (m, 1H), 7.15 (dd, J = 8.4, 1.2 Hz, 1H), 7.20 (d, J = 1.4 Hz, 1H), 7.45 (d, J = 8.4 Hz, 1H), 7.53 (s, 1H), 8.75 (d, J = 7.4 Hz, 1H), 11.59 (s, 1H). Mass spectrum (ESI) m/e = 402 (M + H)+.

    (3S,4R)-tert-Butyl 3-((2-(Dimethylamino)ethyl)carbamoyl)-4-(5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxamido)pyrrolidine-1-carboxylate (46)

    (3S,4R)-1-tert-Butyl 3-methyl 4-(5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxamido)pyrrolidine-1,3-dicarboxylate 45 (1.25 g, 3.00 mmol) was dissolved in THF (10 mL), and a solution of LiOH (288 mg, 12 mmol) in water (10 mL) was added at 0 °C. The mixture was allowed to warm to rt and was stirred overnight. EtOAc was added, and the mixture was washed with 20% aqueous NH4Cl and brine. The organic layer was then dried over Na2SO4 and concentrated in vacuo to afford the corresponding carboxylic acid (800 mg). This was dissolved in DMF (5 mL) and DCM (5 mL), and N1,N1-dimethylethane-1,2-diamine (0.341 g, 3.87 mmol), Et3N (0.540 mL, 3.87 mmol), and HATU (1.472 g, 3.87 mmol) were added to the mixture at 0 °C. The mixture was allowed to warm to rt and stirred overnight. EtOAc was added and the mixture washed with 20% aqueous NaHCO3 and brine. The organic layer was then dried over Na2SO4 and concentrated in vacuo to afford compound 46 (1.00 g, 73%). Mass spectrum (ESI) m/e = 458 (M + H)+.

    (3S,4R)-Methyl 4-(5-Methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxamido)-1-(phenylsulfonyl)pyrrolidine-3-carboxylate (47)

    (3S,4R)-1-tert-Butyl 3-methyl-4-(5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxamido)pyrrolidine-1,3-dicarboxylate 45 (500 mg, 1.25 mmol) was dissolved in MeOH (10 mL). TMSCl (0.159 mL, 1.25 mmol) was added, and the mixture was stirred at rt overnight. The solution was concentrated in vacuo, which afforded the HCl salt of (3S,4R)-methyl 4-(5-methyl-1H-indole-2-carboxamido)pyrrolidine-3-carboxylate (400 mg). This was dissolved in DCM (10 mL), pyridine (1.342 mL, 16.59 mmol) was added to the mixture, and it was cooled to 0 °C. Benzenesulfonyl chloride (586 mg, 3.32 mmol) was then added and mixture allowed to warm to rt and stirred overnight. EtOAc was added, and the solution was washed sequentially with 1 M HCl, sat. NaHCO3, and brine. The organic phase was then dried over Na2SO4 and purified by column chromatography (EtOAc/pentane, 20%), which afforded the desired compound 47 (200 mg, 36%). Mass spectrum (ESI) m/e = 442 (M + H)+.

    (3S,4R)-Methyl 4-(((S)-1-Phenylethyl)amino)-1-(phenylsulfonyl)pyrrolidine-3-carboxylate (48)

    (S)-tert-Butyl 3-((3S,4R)-4-Amino-1-(phenylsulfonyl)pyrrolidine-3-carboxamido)piperidine-1-carboxylate (50)

    Pd(OH)2/C (150 mg, 1.07 mmol) was added to a solution of (S)-tert-butyl 3-((3S,4R)-4-(((S)-1-phenylethyl)amino)-1-(phenylsulfonyl)pyrrolidine-3-carboxamido)piperidine-1-carboxylate 49 (1 g, 1.80 mmol) in EtOH (20 mL) under nitrogen.     Methyl (3S,4R)-1-(Benzenesulfonyl)-4-[methyl-[(1S)-1-phenylethyl]amino]pyrrolidine-3-carboxylate (51)

    To a solution of (3S,4R)-methyl 4-(((S)-1-phenylethyl)amino)-1-(phenylsulfonyl)pyrrolidine-3-carboxylate 48 (270 mg, 0.70 mmol) in formic acid (2666 μL, 69.50 mmol) was added formaldehyde (517 μL, 6.95 mmol).     Methyl (3S,4R)-1-(Benzenesulfonyl)-4-(methylamino)pyrrolidine-3-carboxylate (52)

    To a solution of (3S,4R)-methyl 4-(methyl((S)-1-phenylethyl)amino)-1-(phenylsulfonyl)pyrrolidine-3-carboxylate, HCl 51 (0.27 g, 0.62 mmol) in MeOH (20 mL), Pd/C (0.033 g, 0.03 mmol) was added under nitrogen. The mixture was stirred under 4 bar hydrogen for 3 h.     Methyl (3S,4R)-1-(Benzenesulfonyl)-4-[[5-(2-chlorophenyl)isoxazole-3-carbonyl]methylamino]pyrrolidine-3-carboxylate (53)

    Methyl (3S,4R)-1-(benzenesulfonyl)-4-(methylamino)pyrrolidine-3-carboxylate 52 was dissolved in DMF (4 mL), and then 5-(2-chlorophenyl)isoxazole-3-carboxylic acid (0.144 g, 0.65 mmol) and N-methylmorpholine (0.149 mL, 1.35 mmol) were added, followed by HATU (0.281 g, 0.74 mmol).     trans-Dimethyl 1-Benzyl-3-methylpyrrolidine-3,4-dicarboxylate (55)

    A solution of dimethyl 2-methylfumarate (1 mL, 7.02 mmol) and N-benzyl-1-methoxy-N-((trimethylsilyl)methyl)methanamine (1.796 mL, 7.02 mmol) in anhydrous toluene (5 mL) was cooled to 0 °C under nitrogen. To this mixture TFA was added (0.541 mL, 7.02 mmol) dropwise over 15 min. The mixture was stirred at 0 °C for a further 15 min and then quenched by addition of NH4Cl solution (5 mL). After 15 min, the mixture was diluted with water (50 mL), acidified with 2 M HCl, and extracted with TBME (50 mL). The aqueous phase was adjusted to pH > 10 by addition of NaOH and extracted with TBME (50 mL).     trans-Dimethyl 3-Methyl-1-(phenylsulfonyl)pyrrolidine-3,4-dicarboxylate (56)

    A solution of trans-dimethyl 1-benzyl-3-methylpyrrolidine-3,4-dicarboxylate 55 (0.98 g, 3.36 mmol) in MeOH (20 mL) was flushed thoroughly with nitrogen, and then Pd(OH)2/C (0.472 g, 3.36 mmol) was added. The mixture was hydrogenated at 4 bar for 24 h, then filtered through a Celite pad, washed with MeOH, and the filtrate was evaporated in vacuo to dryness. Benzenesulfonyl chloride (0.477 mL, 3.70 mmol) was added and the resulting 2-phase mixture stirred rapidly at rt for 18 h. The mixture was diluted with water (15 mL) and extracted with EtOAc (2 × 30 mL). Mass spectrum (ESI) m/e = 342 (M + H)+.

    Mixture of trans-1-(Benzenesulfonyl)-4-methoxycarbonyl-3-methyl-pyrrolidine-3-carboxylic Acid (57a) and trans-1-(Benzenesulfonyl)-4-methoxycarbonyl-4-methylpyrrolidine-3-carboxylic Acid (57b)

    A solution of trans-dimethyl 3-methyl-1-(phenylsulfonyl)pyrrolidine-3,4-dicarboxylate 56 (0.81 g, 2.38 mmol) in THF (10 mL) and MeOH (2.5 mL) was cooled to 0 °C, and 1 M NaOH solution (2.38 mL, 2.38 mmol) was added. The mixture was allowed to warm to 10 °C, stirred for 3 h, then warmed to rt and stirred for 20 h. The reaction mixture was then cooled to 0 °C, and further 1 M NaOH solution (1.19 mL, 1.19 mmol) was added. After 1 h the mixture was diluted with water (20 mL), acidified with 2 M HCl, and extracted with EtOAc (2 × 20 mL). The organic extracts were dried (MgSO4), filtered, and evaporated in vacuo to give a crude mixture of compounds 57a and 57b (0.64 g, 82% crude). Mass spectrum (ESI) (−ve) = 326 (M – H)−.

    Mixture of trans-Methyl-1-(benzenesulfonyl)-4-(benzyloxycarbonylamino)-4-methyl-pyrrolidine-3-carboxylate (58a) and trans-Methyl-1-(benzenesulfonyl)-4-(benzyloxycarbonylamino)-3-methyl-pyrrolidine-3-carboxylate (58b)

    A mixture of regioisomers 57a and 57b (630 mg, 1.92 mmol), diphenyl phosphorazidate (0.456 mL, 2.12 mmol), and Et3N (0.295 mL, 2.12 mmol) in dry toluene (20 mL) were heated to reflux under nitrogen for 8 h. Benzyl alcohol (1.0 mL, 9.62 mmol) was added, and the mixture was refluxed for 1 h. The mixture was then cooled to rt, diluted with NaHCO3 aq solution, and extracted with EtOAc (20 mL). The organics were dried (MgSO4), filtered, and evaporated in vacuo. The residue was purified by column chromatography (20–100% EtOAc/heptane) to give a mixture of compounds 58a and 58b (443 mg, 53% crude). Mass spectrum (ESI) m/e = 433 (M + H)+.

    Mixture of tert-Butyl (3S)-3-[trans-1-(Benzenesulfonyl)-4-(benzyloxycarbonylamino)-4-methyl-pyrrolidine-3-carbonyl]amino]piperidine-1-carboxylate (59a) and tert-Butyl (3S)-3-[trans-1-(Benzenesulfonyl)-4-(benzyloxycarbonylamino)-3-methylpyrrolidine-3-carbonyl]amino]piperidine-1-carboxylate (59b)

    To a solution of the mixture of the regioisomers 58a and 58b (443 mg, 1.02 mmol) in THF (20 mL) and MeOH (4 mL), 1 M NaOH solution (4.10 mL, 4.10 mmol) was added. The mixture was heated to reflux for 15 min, cooled to rt, diluted with water (40 mL), acidified with 2 M HCl, extracted with EtOAc (2 × 20 mL), dried (MgSO4), filtered, and evaporated in vacuo. This was dissolved in DMF (4 mL), and Et3N (0.175 mL, 1.26 mmol) and (S)-tert-butyl 3-aminopiperidine-1-carboxylate (231 mg, 1.15 mmol) were added, followed by HATU (438 mg, 1.15 mmol). After 30 min, the mixture was diluted with NaHCO3 aq solution (20 mL), stirred for 2 h, and then extracted with EtOAc (2 × 20 mL). The organic extracts were dried (MgSO4), filtered, and evaporated. The residue was dissolved in EtOH (20 mL), the flask was flushed with nitrogen, and Pd(OH)2/C (36.7 mg, 0.05 mmol) was added. The resulting mixture was stirred under 4 bar hydrogen at rt for 3 days. The catalyst was removed by filtration through a small pad of Celite, washing with EtOH (3 × 5 mL). The filtrate was evaporated in vacuo and then the residue coevaporated with THF (10 mL) to give a mixture of the regioisomers 59a and 59b (480 mg). Mass spectrum (ESI) m/e = 467 (M + H)+.

    Biological Evaluation. Protocol for Ghrelin Receptor Binding Assay

    The assay detects compound binding to the GHS-R1a receptor when competing with [125I]-ghrelin in membranes prepared from HEK293s cell line. Test compounds or DMSO (1 μL per well, final DMSO concentration 0.5%) were plated in 96-well assay plates (Greiner V-bottom PP). 150 μL of cell membranes (final concentration 8 μg/well) in assay buffer (50 mM Tris, 5 mM MgCl2, 0.01% BSA; pH 7.4) supplemented with methyl arachidonyl fluorophosphonate (MAFP, 1 μg/well; Sigma) was added to the plates. 50 μL of [His[125I]]-h ghrelin (final concentration 75 pM; PerkinElmer) in assay buffer was added followed by 1 h incubation at room temperature. The bound and unbound isotope was separated by filtration, using polyethyleneimine (PEI) pretreated (200 μL of 50% PEI per well; Aldrich) filter plates (96-well Millipore, catalog no. MSFCNXB50). Following filtration the plates were washed 8 times with 200 μL/well of ice-cold wash buffer (50 mM Tris, 5 mM MgCl2, 50 mM NaCl; pH 7.4). The amount of membrane-bound radioligand was then quantified (Wallac Trilux Microbeta). Effect of the compounds was expressed as a percentage of maximal effect of (d-Arg1, d-Phe5, d-Trp7,9, Leu11)-SubstanceP. The concentration and percent effect of the test compound were fitted using a sigmoidal dose–response model (Genedata Screener), and potency (IC50) was determined. The results obtained in this assay are shown in Tables 1–4.

    Protocol for Ghrelin IP1 Agonist Assay

    In HEK293s cells stably expressing human GHS receptor,(39) agonist activity was assessed by measuring intracellular myo-inositol 1 phosphate (IP1) using the IP-One Tb (HTRF) assay kit (Cisbio International).(40,41) Solutions were prepared as described by the supplier. Test compounds or DMSO (200 nL per well, final DMSO concentration 1%) were plated in 384-well assay plates (Greiner Lumitrac 200). Cryopreserved cells were thawed, washed in Dulbecco’s modified Eagle medium (DMEM; Invitrogen) containing 10% fetal calf serum (FCS; Hyclone), and resuspended in stimulation buffer (10 mM HEPES, 1 mM CaCl2, 0.5 mM MgCl2, 4.2 mM KCl, 146 mM NaCl, 5.5 mM glucose, 50 mM LiCl, pH 7.4; supplied in the assay kit) supplemented with 0.02% bovine serum albumin (BSA; Sigma) to a density of 0.75 × 106 cells/mL. Cell suspension (20 μL/well) was added to the compound plate and incubated for 90 min at 37 °C in 5% CO2/95% air. Following lysis of cells using lysis buffer (supplied in the assay kit) in the presence of IP1-d2 (5 μL/well) and anti-IP1 cryptate (5 μL/well) for 60 min at room temperature, fluorescence was measured on the Pherastar (BMG Labtech) at 612 and 665 nm, respectively. The effect of the compounds was expressed as a percentage of maximal effect of GHRP-6 (5 μM; Bachem). The concentration and percent effect of the test compound were fitted using a sigmoidal dose–response model (Genedata Screener), and potency (EC50) and efficacy (% agonism) were determined. Compounds were considered active in the agonist assay when efficacy was >30% and as full agonists when efficacy was >85%. The results obtained in this assay are shown in Tables 1–4, 8, 9.
    The dog IP1 agonist assay was run according to the same protocol using HEK293s cells stably expressing the dog GHS receptor. Cell density was 0.5 × 106 cells/mL, and the compound incubation time was 60 min. The effect of the compounds was expressed as a percentage of maximal effect of capromorelin (3.33 μM; Apac). The results obtained in this assay is shown in Table 7.

    Animal Study Protocol

    The present study was approved by the local ethical committee in Gothenburg (2010-04-01).

    Experimental Procedures

    All dogs were fed at 14:30 each day, starting at least 1 week before the first experiment and after the last sample on study day. All dogs were weighed and orally dosed at 7:30. Blood samples were obtained before dosing, 15, 30, and 45 min and 1, 2, 4, 7, 12, and 24 h after dose. The blood was collected in K2E EDTA coated BD Vacutainer 2 mL vials and centrifuged at 4 °C, 3.0g for 5 min, and 125 μL of plasma was transferred to 0.5 mL Eppendorf tubes or 96-well plates (compound level) and kept frozen (<−20 °C compound level, <−80 °C, IGF-1) until analysis. The total blood volume obtained was minimized for each blood sample. The maximally allowed volume was 13 mL per experiment.

    Bioanalytical Procedures

    IGF-1 was determined with an IGF-1 ELISA kit purchased from Mediagnost, Germany, catalog no. E20. The assay is performed according to the manufacturer’s instructions. The primary readout is the change in IGF-1 levels from baseline in relation to plasma levels of the compound. Paired student T test is used for statistical check at each time point. Changes with P values below 5% were considered to be significant.

      1
      Ghrelin-producing cells exist as two types of cells, closed- and opened-type cells, in the rat gastrointestinal tract
      Sakata, Ichiro; Nakamura, Kazuaki; Yamazaki, Mami; Matsubara, Maki; Hayashi, Yuijiro; Kangawa, Kenji; Sakai, Takafumi
      Peptides (New York, NY, United States) (2002), 23 (3), 531-536CODEN: PPTDD5; ISSN:0196-9781. (Elsevier Science Inc.)
      Ghrelin was recently isolated from the rat stomach as an endogenous ligand for the growth-hormone secretagogue receptor (GHS-R) and is known to exist in the gastrointestinal tract and hypothalamus. In this study, the authors investigated in detail the distribution and morphol. characteristics of ghrelin-contg. cells (ghrelin cells) in the gastrointestinal tract by immunohistochem. and in situ hybridization. Ghrelin cells were found to be localized in the mucous membrane of the stomach, duodenum, ileum, cecum and colon but not in myenteric plexus, and they can be classified into open- and closed-type cells. The greatest no. of ghrelin cells was found in the stomach, and it was found that the no. of the opened-type cells gradually increased in the direction from stomach to the lower gastrointestinal tract. These results suggest that the two types of ghrelin cells may be distinctly regulated and play different physiol. roles in various regions of the gastrointestinal tract.

      The Homeostatic Force of Ghrelin
      Yanagi, Shigehisa; Sato, Takahiro; Kangawa, Kenji; Nakazato, Masamitsu
      Cell Metabolism (2018), 27 (4), 786-804CODEN: CMEEB5; ISSN:1550-4131. (Elsevier Inc.)
      A review. Ghrelin, a gastric-derived acylated peptide, regulates energy homeostasis by transmitting information about peripheral nutritional status to the brain, and is essential for protecting organisms against famine. Ghrelin operates brain circuits to regulate homeostatic and hedonic feeding. Recent research advances have shed new light on ghrelin's multifaceted roles in cellular homeostasis, which could maintain the internal environment and overcome metaflammation in metabolic organs. Here, we highlight our current understanding of the regulatory mechanisms of the ghrelin system in energy metab. and cellular homeostasis and its clin. trials. Future studies of ghrelin will further elucidate how the stomach regulates systemic homeostasis.

In recent years, ghrelin has been found to have a plethora of central and peripheral actions in distinct areas including learning and memory, gut motility and gastric acid secretion, sleep/wake rhythm, reward seeking behavior, taste sensation and glucose metab.

      Ghrelin receptor inverse agonists as a novel therapeutic approach against obesity-related metabolic disease
      Abegg, Kathrin; Bernasconi, Lara; Hutter, Melanie; Whiting, Lynda; Pietra, Claudio; Giuliano, Claudio; Lutz, Thomas A.; Riediger, Thomas
      Diabetes, Obesity and Metabolism (2017), 19 (12), 1740-1750CODEN: DOMEF6; ISSN:1462-8902. (Wiley-Blackwell)
      Ghrelin is implicated in the control of energy balance and glucose homeostasis. The ghrelin receptor exhibits ligand-independent constitutive activity, which can be pharmacol. exploited to induce inverse ghrelin actions. Because ghrelin receptor inverse agonists (GHSR-IA) might be effective for the treatment of obesity-related metabolic disease, we tested 2 novel synthetic compds. GHSR-IA1 and GHSR-IA2. In functional cell assays, electrophysiogical and immunohistochem. expts., we demonstrated inverse agonist activity for GHSR-IA1 and GHSR-IA2. We used healthy mice, Zucker diabetic fatty (ZDF) rats and diet-induced obese (DIO) mice to explore effects on food intake (FI), body wt. (BW), conditioned taste aversion (CTA), oral glucose tolerance (OGT), pancreatic islet morphol., hepatic steatosis (HS), and blood lipids. Both compds. acutely reduced FI in mice without inducing CTA. Chronic GHSR-IA1 increased metabolic rate in chow-fed mice, suppressed FI, and improved OGT in ZDF rats. Moreover, the progression of islet hyperplasia to fibrosis in ZDF rats slowed down. GHSR-IA2 reduced FI and BW in DIO mice, and reduced fasting and stimulated glucose levels compared with pair-fed and vehicle-treated mice. GHSR-IA2-treated DIO mice showed decreased blood lipids. GHSR-IA1 treatment markedly decreased HS in DIO mice. Our study demonstrates therapeutic actions of novel ghrelin receptor inverse agonists, suggesting a potential to treat obesity-related metabolic disorders including diabetes mellitus.

      Rational Design, Synthesis, and Pharmacological Characterization of Novel Ghrelin Receptor Inverse Agonists as Potential Treatment against Obesity-Related Metabolic Diseases
      Daina, Antoine; Giuliano, Claudio; Pietra, Claudio; Wang, Junbo; Chi, Yushi; Zou, Zack; Li, Fugang; Yan, Zhonghua; Zhou, Yifan; Guainazzi, Angelo; Garcia Rubio, Silvina; Zoete, Vincent
      Journal of Medicinal Chemistry (2018), 61 (24), 11039-11060CODEN: JMCMAR; ISSN:0022-2623. (American Chemical Society)
      A new chemotype of ghrelin inverse agonists was discovered through chimeric design based on mol. scaffolds known as growth-hormone secretagogue receptor (GHSR) modulators but with divergent pharmacodynamic and pharmacokinetic properties. The structure-activities/properties exploration led to compd. I, which displayed potent human GHSR antagonism and inverse agonism in cellular assays (IC50 = 68 nM, EC50 = 29 nM), moderate oral bioavailability, and notable brain penetration in rat (F = 27%, B/P ratio = 1.9). First in vivo studies demonstrated effective redn. of food intake after oral or parenteral administration to mouse (78% at 1 h and 38% at 8 h, resp.). Further preclin. studies are needed to evaluate the most suited mode of administration with the aim of promoting a first central-acting ghrelin inverse agonist mol. to development, which would represent a significant step toward therapeutic agents to treat metabolic disorders related to obesity, such as type 2 diabetes mellitus.

      The 1,2,4-triazole as a scaffold for the design of ghrelin receptor ligands: development of JMV 2959, a potent antagonist
      Moulin, Aline; Brunel, Luc; Boeglin, Damien; Demange, Luc; Ryan, Johanne; M'Kadmi, Celine; Denoyelle, Severine; Martinez, Jean; Fehrentz, Jean-Alain
      Amino Acids (2013), 44 (2), 301-314CODEN: AACIE6; ISSN:0939-4451. (SpringerWienNewYork)
      Ghrelin is a 28-residue peptide acylated with an n-octanoyl group on the Ser 3 residue, predominantly produced by the stomach. Ghrelin displays strong growth hormone (GH) releasing activity, which is mediated by the activation of the so-called GH secretagogue receptor type 1a (GHS-R1a). Given the wide spectrum of biol. activities of Ghrelin in neuroendocrine and metabolic pathways, many research groups, including our group, developed synthetic peptide, and nonpeptide GHS-R1a ligands, acting as agonists, partial agonists, antagonists, or inverse agonists. In this highlight article, we will focus on the discovery of a GHS-R1a antagonist compd., JMV 2959, which has been extensively studied in different in vitro and in vivo models. We will first describe the peptidomimetic approach that led us to discover this compd. Then we will review the results obtained with this compd. in different studies in the fields of food intake and obesity, addictive behaviors, hyperactivity and retinopathy.

      7
      Treatment of cachexia with ghrelin in patients with COPD
      Nagaya, Noritoshi; Itoh, Takefumi; Murakami, Shinsuke; Oya, Hideo; Uematsu, Masaaki; Miyatake, Kunio; Kangawa, Kenji
      Chest (2005), 128 (3), 1187-1193CODEN: CHETBF; ISSN:0012-3692. (American College of Chest Physicians)
      Study objectives: Ghrelin is a novel growth hormone (GH)-releasing peptide that also induces a pos. energy balance by decreasing fat utility and stimulating feeding through GH-independent mechanisms. We investigated whether ghrelin improves cachexia and functional capacity in patients with COPD. Methods: This is an open-label pilot study. Human ghrelin (2 μg/kg bid) was IV administered to seven cachectic patients with COPD for 3 wk. Food intake, body compn., muscle strength, exercise capacity, pulmonary function, and sympathetic nerve activity were examd. before and after ghrelin therapy. Results: A single administration of ghrelin markedly increased serum GH (21-fold). Three-week treatment with ghrelin resulted in a significant increase in mean (± SEM) body wt. (49.3 ± 3.6 to 50.3 ± 3.8 kg; p < 0.05). Food intake was significantly increased during ghrelin therapy. Ghrelin increased lean body mass and peripheral and respiratory muscle strength. Ghrelin significantly increased Karnofsky performance status score and the distance walked in 6 min (370 ± 30 to 432 ± 35 m; p < 0.05), although it did not significantly alter pulmonary function. Ghrelin attenuated the exaggerated sympathetic nerve activity, as indicated by a marked decrease in plasma norepinephrine level (889 ± 123 to 597 ± 116 pg/mL; p < 0.05). Conclusions: These preliminary results suggest that repeated administration of ghrelin improves body compn., muscle wasting, functional capacity, and sympathetic augmentation in cachectic patients with COPD.

      Identification and pharmacological profile of an indane-based series of Ghrelin receptor full agonists
      Gardelli, Cristina; Wada, Hiroki; Ray, Asim; Caffrey, Moya; Llinas, Antonio; Shamovsky, Igor; Tholander, Joakim; Larsson, Joakim; Sivars, Ulf; Hultin, Leif; Andersson, Ulf; Sanganee, Hitesh J.; Stenvall, Kristina; Leidvik, Brith; Gedda, Karin; Jinton, Lisa; Ryden Landergren, Marie; Karabelas, Kostas
      Journal of Medicinal Chemistry (2018), 61 (14), 5974-5987CODEN: JMCMAR; ISSN:0022-2623. (American Chemical Society)
      Cachexia and muscle wasting are very common among patients suffering from cancer, chronic obstructive pulmonary disease, and other chronic diseases. Ghrelin stimulates growth hormone secretion via the ghrelin receptor, which subsequently leads to increase of IGF-1 plasma levels. The activation of the GH/IGF-1 axis leads to an increase of muscle mass and functional capacity. Ghrelin further acts on inflammation, appetite, and adipogenesis and for this reason was considered an important target to address catabolic conditions. We report the synthesis and properties of an indane based series of ghrelin receptor full agonists; they have been shown to generate a sustained increase of IGF-1 levels in dog and have been thoroughly investigated with respect to their functional activity.

      Ghrelin is an appetite-stimulatory signal from stomach with structural resemblance to motilin
      Asakawa, Akihiro; Inui, Akio; Kaga, Toshihiro; Yuzuriha, Hideki; Nagata, Toshiaki; Ueno, Naohiko; Makino, Susumu; Fujimiya, Mineko; Niijima, Akira; Fujino, Masayuki A.; Kasuga, Masato
      Gastroenterology (2001), 120 (2), 337-345CODEN: GASTAB; ISSN:0016-5085. (W. B. Saunders Co.)
      Ghrelin, an endogenous ligand for growth hormone secretagogue receptor, was recently identified in the rat stomach. The authors examd. the effects of the gastric peptide ghrelin on energy balance in assocn. with leptin and vagal nerve activity. Food intake, oxygen consumption, gastric emptying, and hypothalamic neuropeptide Y (NPY) mRNA expression were measured after intra-third cerebroventricular or i.p. injections of ghrelin in mice. The gastric vagal nerve activity was recorded after i.v. administration in rats. Gastric ghrelin gene expression was assessed by Northern blot anal. Repeated coadministration of ghrelin and interleukin (IL)-1β was continued for 5 days. Ghrelin exhibited gastroprokinetic activity with structural resemblance to motilin and potent orexigenic activity through action on the hypothalamic neuropeptide Y (NPY) and Y1 receptor, which was lost after vagotomy. Ghrelin decreased gastric vagal afferent discharge in contrast to other anorexigenic peptides that increased the activity. Ghrelin gene expression in the stomach was increased by fasting and in ob/ob mice, and was decreased by administration of leptin and IL-1β. Peripherally administered ghrelin blocked IL-1β-induced anorexia and produced pos. energy balance by promoting food intake and decreasing energy expenditure. Ghrelin, which is neg. regulated by leptin and IL-1β, is secreted by the stomach and increases arcuate NPY expression, which in turn acts through Y1 receptors to increase food intake and decrease energy expenditure. Gastric peptide ghrelin may thus function as part of the orexigenic pathway downstream from leptin and is a potential therapeutic target not only for obesity but also for anorexia and cachexia.
